HIV/AIDS Outreach in Southern New Mexico: From Design to Implementation

Author: Valverde, Michelle1; Felderman-Taylor, Jennifer1 Dept Sociology and Anthropology, New Mexico State U, P.O. Box 30001, Las Cruces. E-mail: mvalverde@zianet.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61390443?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper describes the outreach component of the New Mexico Border Health Initiative (NMBHI), one of five Special Projects of National Significance funded in 2000 along the United States/Mexico border by the Health Resources and Services Administration. The initial intent of the NMBHI was to utilize the traditional promotor model of natural helpers within the informal support systems in the communities in which they physically live for the purpose of increasing utilization of HIV/AIDS prevention services. What evolved instead was more closely aligned with traditional street outreach, whereby peers were utilized to engage and inform potential clients about HIV prevention, provide HIV testing and link HIV-affected persons to medical services. Programmatic elements, implications for practice, and recommendations for program coordinators are presented in the paper. Adapted from the source document. When It Happens to Your Family: One Social Worker's Response

Author: Thomas, Norma D11 Center Ethnic & Minority Aging, Philadelphia, PA cemaorg@aol.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61376101?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article is a case study of how an African American family that includes a social worker, responded when three members received an HIV/AIDS diagnosis; two of whom are now deceased. It provides a retrospective look at a family's response to the diagnosis & resilience in the face of terminal illness. This is a personal story presented originally at the HIV/AIDS conference titled "A Social Work Response," held in Los Angeles, California in 1997. The article provides insight to practitioners as well as offers a comparative experience for those who have also passed along on a similar journey. This family faced terminal illness many times, but until one of the matriarchs was diagnosed with AIDS in 1989, this was always an illness that happened to someone else's family. References. Facilitators and Barriers to Care for Haitian Americans with HIV or AIDS

Author: Barsky, Allan E1; Albertini, Velmarie L1 Florida Atlantic University, School of Social Work, Fort Lauderdale, FL E-mail: barsky@barsky.org

Mothers Disclosure of Maternal HIV Status to Children: Key Psychosocial Correlates

Author: Letteney, Susan11 Social Work Progam, York Coll City U New York, Jamaica letteney@york.cuny.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61382597?accountid=14925

Abstract: The present study examines the relationship between maternal disclosure of HIV status to children & key psychosocial variables. The article identifies three key psychosocial characteristics that may play a role in maternal HIV status disclosure to children: HIV-related perceptions of stigma, HIV-related stigma management, & social support. Eighty-eight (88) HIV women with children enrolled in outpatient care at an urban medical center completed the HIV-Related Perceptions of Stigma & HIV-Related Stigma Management measures, the Profile of Mood States (POMS), & items measuring social support & disclosure to children. Mothers who disclosed their HIV status to their children reported significantly fewer perceptions of HIV-related stigma, less use of concealment to manage HIV-related stigma, & more social support than mothers who did not disclose to their children. No differences were found between disclosure groups for physical or psychological distress. The perception of stigma attached to a mother's HIV positive status & strategies for managing this stigma emerged as the principal findings of the present study. These findings provide empirical support for identifying stigma & its management as a central focus for professionals working in the field of HIV & families. Additional empirical research is needed to fully understand the factors that influence maternal HIV status disclosure to children. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. Thai Women's Experiences with HIV/AIDS: Perspectives on Coping

Author: Sorajjakool, Siroj11 Griggs Hall, Loma Linda University, Loma Linda, CA E-mail: ssorajjakool@rel.llu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61422031?accountid=14925

Abstract: This qualitative study is based on interviews with 10 Thai women living with HIV/AIDS in northern Thailand. It is an attempt to learn how individuals who have no access to mental health facilities cope with distress in life. The analysis shows three important factors that enhance coping ability: support and acceptance from families and friends, life purpose, and religious beliefs (Buddhism) pertaining to the acceptance of mortality. It concludes by suggesting the need to recognize other ways of coping beyond the scope of traditional Western psychotherapeutic approaches. Adapted from the source document. An Exploratory Study of HIV Prevention with Mexican/Latino Migrant Day Laborers

Author: Organista, Kurt C1; Alvarado, Nicholas J; Balblutin-Burnham, Amity; Worby, Paula; Martinez, Sergio R1 U California, Berkeley, CA. E-mail: DRKCO@Berkeley.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61384513?accountid=14925

Abstract: The purpose of this exploratory study was to develop, implement, and evaluate a pilot HIV prevention intervention with one of the most mobile of U.S.-Mexico transborder populations: Mexican/Latino migrant day laborers (MDLs). Intervention development was informed by preliminary research that included an HIV risk survey of over a hundred MDLs, and a focus group to explore the topic of HIV in the lives of MDLs. Both quantitative and qualitative methods were used to evaluate the intervention, and to identify some of the contextual characteristics of HIV risk factors in the MDL experience. For example, the most frequent theme revealed by qualitative analysis was the stressful and vulnerable state of desesperacion [desperation], resulting from earning too little money, that participants linked to deviating from their migration goals and succumbing to alcohol and drug use, and risky sex. While empirical results are limited by the small sample of convenience (N = 23), lack of a control group, and loss of about half of the sample by one month follow-up evaluation, findings were encouragingly consistent with intervention goals: Post-intervention data revealed what appear to be substantial gains in carrying condoms (e.g., from 43% to 83%) as well as in knowledge of proper condom use. Further, frequency of sex with risky sex partners decreased in general, while condom use was reported for all sexual encounters assessed during post-evaluation. The theoretical framework used to guide the intervention, a hybrid of the Health Belief Model and Friere's model of participatory education, included visual triggers composed of customized Mexican lottery game cards to elicit discussion of HIV risk and prevention in the MDL experience. Adapted from the source document. COPIES ARE AVAILABLE FROM: HAWORTH DOCUMENT DELIVERY CENTER, The Haworth Press, Inc., 10 Alice Street, Binghamton, NY 13904-1580

Continuing to Provide and Fund a Mix of Ryan White Title I HIV/AIDS Services: Support from "The Voices of Experience" Needs Surveys in Massachusetts

Author: Beinecke, Richard H1; Matava, Marie A; Rivers, Nicole; Stevens, Richard; Goldrosen, Michael; Averbach, Abigail R; Woliver, Renee; Kelley, Betsy1 Suffolk University Department of Public Management, 8 Ashburton Place, Boston, MA E-mail: rickdeb61@aol.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61425301?accountid=14925

Abstract: Consumer Research Associates from the Suffolk University Center for Public Management conducted three needs surveys in 1997, 2000, and 2005 titled "Voices of Experience," with approximately 450 peer interviews conducted each year. These surveys were used to assess changing service needs, outcomes, and consumer satisfaction for people living with HIV/AIDS (PLWH) and to monitor service delivery in the Boston Eligible Metropolitan Area (EMA) located in Eastern Massachusetts and Southern New Hampshire of the Ryan White Care Act Title I and Massachusetts Title II and state-funded programs. The assessments support the importance of continuing to adequately fund a broad mix of services to support the medical and social service needs of persons living with HIV/AIDS. A Sexual Risk Reduction Intervention for Female Sex Workers in Mexico: Design and Baseline Characteristics

Author: Patterson, Thomas L1; Semple, Shirley J; Fraga, Miguel; Bucardo, Jesus; de la Torre, Adela; Salazar-Reyna, Juan; Orozovich, Prisci; Orozco, Hugo Salvador Staines; Amaro, Hortensia; Magis-Rodriguez, Carlos; Strathdee, Steffanie A1 U California, San Diego, School of Medicine, Dept Psychiatry, La Jolla, CA.

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61362954?accountid=14925

Abstract: Female sex workers (FSWs) 18 or older who reported having unprotected sex with at least one client within the previous month were recruited in Tijuana and Ciudad Juarez, Mexico. After a baseline assessment, participants were randomly assigned to either: (1) Proyecto Comparte Sexo Mas Seguro ("Share Safer Sex"), a theory-based counseling intervention to increase the use of condoms; or (2) time-equivalent voluntary HIV counseling and testing. Participants had unprotected sex with approximately 27% of clients over the one-month reporting period, and approximately 5% of FSWs in both study conditions tested HIV-seropositive. FSWs in the intervention and comparison conditions showed no significant differences in baseline demographic characteristics, sexual risk behaviors, or HIV serostatus, which indicates that randomization was successful. Future challenges entail participant follow-up and evaluation of intervention effects. Adapted from the source document. Informal Social Support for HIV Medical Care

Author: Waddell, Elizabeth Needham1; Messeri, Peter1 Heilbrunn Department of Population and Family Health, Mailman School of Public Health, B-2, New York, NY E-mail: ecn5@columbia.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61405365?accountid=14925

Abstract: The purpose of this paper is to explore the relationship of availability of HIV-related information and emotional support necessary to obtaining appropriate medical care. Multivariate models control for use of professional case management, mental health services, and drug treatment services. Study data were obtained from the first two rounds of interviews from the New York City Community Health Advisory and Information Network (CHAIN). Household information support and professional ancillary services were significantly associated with entry into appropriate medical care. Among participants in appropriate care at the baseline, only professional services were significantly associated with continuity of care. Adapted from the source document. A Transcultural Case Management Model for HIV/AIDS Care and Prevention

Author: Ramos, Rebeca L1; Ferreira-Pinto, Joao B1 US Mexico Border Health Association. E-mail: rebeca@utep.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61384567?accountid=14925

Abstract: The Transcultural Case Management (TCM) intervention utilizing community health-care workers-Promotores- was designed to increase adherence to Highly Active Anti-Retroviral Therapy (HAART) treatment. The TCM model was also expected to help reduce the use of hospital emergency rooms, the number of hospitalization days, and consequently to lower treatment costs among Hispanic HIV-positive male patients. The intervention results, although not statistically significant because of a higher than expected attrition rate and difficulties in accessing patients for follow-up, were in the expected direction. Furthermore, the self-reported quality of life of the patients increased by the development of social networks among patients and by an increased sense of self-reliance and empowerment. Problems encountered in fulfilling the goals of the research protocol, and recommendations on how to improve research in HIV care organizations are also discussed. Adapted from the source document. Organizational Correlates of Specialized Substance Abuse Treatment Provision to Seropositive Clients: A Political Economy Perspective

Author: Ghose, Toorjo11 CIRA, Yale University, Suite 1B, New Haven, CT E-mail: Toorjo.Ghose@yale.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61422049?accountid=14925

Abstract: Data from the Alcohol and Drug Services Study, 1996-1999 (United States Department of Health and Human Services [USDHHS], 2002), a nationally representative sample of 2,351 drug treatment programs were examined to identify organizational predictors of specialized substance abuse treatment provision to seropositive clients. The Political Economy Perspective developed by Wamsley and Zald (1976) was used to interpret results. A minority of facilities (22%, 517) provided specialized treatment. Among internal political economy measures, provision of mental health (p <0.01), basic HIV services (p <0.05), as well as methadone maintenance treatment (p < 0.05) were significantly and positively associated with specialized treatment provision. Among external political economy measures, managed care funding significantly decreased the likelihood of specialized treatment provision (p < 0.05), while parent organization control (p <0.01) and number of clients (p < 0.01) significantly increased it. Specialized treatment provision was significantly positively associated with proportion of Blacks, Hispanics, seropositives (p < 0.01 for all), and injection drug users (p < 0.05). Social Service Delivery Preferences Among African American Women Who Use Crack Cocaine: What Women Say They Need Before They Can Be Open to HIV Prevention Services?

Author: MacMaster, Samuel A11 College of Social Work, University of Tennessee, Nashville, TN E-mail: smacmast@utk.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61422067?accountid=14925

Abstract: African American women involved in high-risk, substance use-related behaviors are affected by extremely high rates of HIV infection. While research has demonstrated that HIV is preventable, in order for prevention efforts to be successful in reducing disparities, perceptions of African American women who use crack cocaine need to be taken into account. This qualitative study presents results of eleven focus groups with eighty-nine African American women respondents presenting their perceptions of service needs and preferences for service delivery. The results indicate two important findings for social service providers: the respondents' lack of a perceived need for HIV prevention services as a spontaneously identified need, and respondents' desires for more basic services such as childcare, safe shelter, basic necessities, and especially substance abuse treatment before they could be approached about HIV prevention services. The findings are relevant for developing gender- and culture-specific prevention strategies to reduce the spread of HIV. Adapted from the source document. The Case for an Integrated Approach to HIV/AIDS Prevention: Support and Treatment Services in Canada

Author: Veinot, Tiffany C11 North Campus Building, Room 240, The University of Western Ontario, London, Ontario, Canada E-mail: tveinot@uwo.ca

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61364091?accountid=14925

Abstract: Worldwide, there is growing policy support for providing integrated HIV/AIDS services. Accordingly, this paper examines the challenges as well as the merits for implementing an integrated approach to HIV/AIDS services in Canada. Through a discussion of the interconnections between HIV/AIDS prevention, support, and treatment, the author outlines how these issues are fundamentally intertwined in the lives of people living with HIV/AIDS (PHAs) and those at risk of HIV infection. This suggests that provision of comprehensive HIV/AIDS services requires approaching these issues from an integrated perspective. The author outlines two existing models of integrated HIV/AIDS services to show the potential viability of such an approach, and concludes with policy recommendations to support service integration. Development of a Community-Based Participatory Research Effort to Evaluate Conventional HIV Testing (CHT) and HIV Rapid Testing (HRT)

Author: Gleason-Comstock, Julie1; Streater, Alicia; Calhoun, Cindy Bolden; Simpson, Harry L; Rolack, Rosalind; Norman, Silas, Jr1 Department of Community Medicine (CM), School of Medicine (SOM), Wayne State University (WSU), Saint Antoine, Detroit, MI E-mail: jgleason@med.wayne.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61422095?accountid=14925

Abstract: A community-based participatory research (CBPR) project was created to study the effects of changing HIV testing protocols on client risk behaviors in an urban area. Concerns raised by the agency advisory group led to development of research questions that needed to be addressed within the community. The article focuses on development of the research partnership and study enrollment during the first year of the project. Challenges of assimilating rapid advances in health technology while conducting outreach to high-risk clients are discussed. Successes and lessons learned are described, including adaptation of theory-based interventions to research protocol and multi-level community coordination. Adapted from the source document. HIV/AIDS Knowledge and Perceptions Among African American Male and Female College Students at a Historical Black University

Author: Mancoske, Ronald J1; Rountree, Michele A; Donovan, Mary Elizabeth; Neighbors, Ira1 Southern University at New Orleans School of Social Work, New Orleans, LA E-mail: rmancosk@suno.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61413655?accountid=14925

Abstract: With the incidence of HIV/AIDS infection continuing to increase among young, African American men and women, it is necessary to examine the breadth of their knowledge on HIV prevention strategies. This study assesses the reported knowledge, attitudes, and behaviors of students attending a Southern urban commuter campus at a Historically Black University. Major differences among males and females, as well as factors influencing testing, issues of safer sex, and perceptions of being HIV infected are examined. The cross-sectional survey data was collected using a self-administered instrument with 238 African American female and male students. Knowledge of transmission was relatively accurate. The majority of students were aware of testing services available to them, though less than 50% of the students reported using services on campus or in their community. There were several significant gender differences in attitudes and knowledge about social supports and condom use. The findings are important for social service providers to consider when developing and/or integrating HIV prevention into a culturally responsive service delivery approach. Regional Approaches to HIV Care and Services

Author: Gilbert, Dorie J1; Linsk, Nathan L1 School Social Work, U Texas, Austin

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61353552?accountid=14925

Abstract: Discusses the use of community based HIV/AIDS programs to meet the needs of a diverse population of residents. The focus of these programs has shifted from HIV/AIDS education and prevention to broader demands fro health promotion, community development, and varied support services. Argues that collaboration efforts between housing, public health, school, child-welfare, and family-based programs need to happen to address the diverse needs of both HIV/AIDS prevention as well as people living with HIV/AIDS.

Developing HIV Service-Enriched Permanent Housing in Five Cities: Perspectives of Social Services Staff

Author: Sotheran, Jo L; Patterson, Elizabeth K; Aidala, Angela A

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61375990?accountid=14925

Abstract: Newer antiretroviral treatments are adding years to the lives of people with HIV. At the same time, the face of the epidemic is changing to include increasing numbers of people with other diagnoses, especially chronic substance use and/or mental illness, that compromise their ability to secure and maintain permanent housing. Social service providers are increasingly attempting to meet the need for permanent supportive housing. However, many social service providers are new to developing specialized permanent housing, and little practical guidance exists. We use the experience of five agencies participating in the Department of Housing and Urban Development's Housing Opportunities for People with AIDS (HOPWA) Multiple Diagnoses Initiative to explore the potential pitfalls and solutions in developing these programs, from the point of view of providers from social service rather than housing backgrounds. An Exploratory Study: HIV/AIDS Educational and Prevention Programming in Domestic Violence Shelters

Author: Rountree, Michele A11 School Social Work, U Texas, Austin

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61387956?accountid=14925

Abstract: The purpose of this exploratory study is to identify the current practices & barriers related to the use of HIV/AIDS risk assessment measures & the provision of HIV/AIDS prevention services in seven domestic violence shelters in Arizona. Interviewees reported several structural impediments to the integration of risk assessment measures & educational/prevention services related to HIV/AIDS including fiscal constraints, enhanced training of staff, hiring of new staff, discrimination by staff & a need for an interdisciplinary approach. Client factors included the stage of recovery potentially interfering with the risk assessment measure process & the behavioral integration of HIV/AIDS education & preventative information. 33 References. Examining the Perceptions of Service Providers for Persons Living with HIV/AIDS in Rural Localities: A Pilot Study

Author: Eastman, Brenda J1; Lieving, Bernard H, III; Bunch, Shelia Grant1 School Social Work, East Carolina U, Greenville, NC eastmanbr@mail.ecu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61394211?accountid=14925

Abstract: There is an increased level of concern that current service structures in rural localities are ill prepared to meet the needs of a growing population of persons infected with HIV/AIDS. Persons living with HIV/AIDS in rural localities face greater stigma, limited accessibility, & restricted resources that further isolate this historically underserved population. This exploratory study utilizes a non-probability purposive sample to investigate the perceptions of service providers in rural regions of North Carolina. Investigators developed & administered a self administered survey comprised of quantitative & qualitative questions targeting service provider perceptions about the general public; perceptions about consumers of services; provider self perceptions, & perceptions related to inter & intra-agency issues. Findings indicate that service providers perceive deficits in public knowledge, social & health services, agency resources, & provider resources. Implications of these findings are discussed as well as the need for continued research efforts. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. The Use of Standardized Patient Encounters for Evaluation of a Clinical Education Program on the Development of HIV/AIDS-Related Clinical Skills

Author: Dieckhaus, Kevin D1; Vontell, Susan; Pfeiffer, Carol; Williams, Ann1 U Connecticut Health Center, Farmington dieckhaus@up.uchc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61388029?accountid=14925

Abstract: The success of medical education is traditionally gauged by performance on written examinations. However, clinical proficiency in real-world situations is difficult to assess by written examinations alone. We developed a novel evaluation tool to measure the development of HIV-specific clinical skills using Standardized Patient Encounters (SPEs). Trainees were evaluated at baseline & after an HIV clinical training module by (1) self-reported confidence with HIV-specific clinical skills, & (2) performance assessments using SPEs. Case scenarios were designed to incorporate unique issues associated with HIV that may present to a primary care provider. SPEs were scored based on written documentation including problem list & plan as well as a review of the videotapes by HIV specialists evaluating key elements of clinical skills & decision making. Improvements were noted in multiple facets of the encounters. The use of SPEs enables evaluation of clinical training interventions on the development of HIV-related clinical skill sets. Supportive Housing for the Chronically Homeless with HIV/AIDS: An Effective Model

Author: Pardasani, Manoj P11 Wurzweiler School Social Work, Yeshiva U, NY mpardasani@doe.org

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61387613?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article examines an innovative supportive housing program for chronically homeless individuals with HIV/AIDS. A Better Place (ABP). a supportive housing program in New York City that provides housing & social services to HIV-positive individuals with multiple diagnoses (mental illness, mental retardation, developmental disability &/or chemical addictions), is offered as an example of an effective & economical model of service that aims to break the cycle of homelessness by providing comprehensive preventative care with positive long-term results. The structure & functioning of the program, along with two case summaries, are described in order to provide a service framework for practitioners engaged in this field of service. 1 Table, 30 References. Look What God Can Do: African-American Gay Men, AIDS and Spirituality

Author: Miller, Robert L11 School Social Welfare, U Albany State U New York, NY rmiller@albany.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61368049?accountid=14925

Abstract: This qualitative study describes how ten African American gay men understand & utilize spirituality while living with AIDS. The narrative data indicate spirituality is a significant strength for these men. The participants describe spirituality, a relationship with God, as interactive, integrative, & protective. They indicate that spirituality helps them live with AIDS & alleviates death anxiety. The participants also posit that their experience with AIDS has strengthened their spirituality. Spirituality is an important resource for the men in this study. The data suggests social workers who work with this population explore the salience of spirituality in the lives of their clients. A Kaleidoscope of Care for HIV-Infected Substance Use

Author: Tobias, Carol1; Brown, Kate; Rajabiun, Serena; Drainoni, Mari-Lynn; Young, Steven R1 Boston U School Public Health, MA tcarol@bu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61370153?accountid=14925

Abstract: The substance abuse treatment & HIV health care systems have different treatment approaches, & many HIV-infected substance users fall through the cracks between systems. Providers are often challenged by the difficulty of engaging & retaining HIV-infected substance users in care & working across disciplines to coordinate services. A Kaleidoscope of Care is a cross-disciplinary curriculum in HIV & substance use, designed to train HIV health care & substance abuse treatment professionals in providing better care to the clients they share in common. It applies adult learning principles to engage diverse professionals in building skills to promote health & adherence, engagement & retention in care, harm reduction, & interdisciplinary collaboration. Early evaluation of the curriculum indicates that it has enhanced knowledge & built skills in serving HIV-infected substance users among providers from multiple disciplines. HIV Education, Prevention, and Outreach Programs in Rural Areas of the Southeastern United States

Author: Zuniga, Miguel A1; Buchanan, Robert J; Chakravorty, Bonnie J1 Dept Health Informatics, School Allied Health Sciences, Medical Coll Georgia, Augusta mzuniga@mcg.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61380437?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study describes HIV education, prevention, and outreach activities, including barriers to providing these services, to clients in rural areas of Alabama, Louisiana, Mississippi, North Carolina, and South Carolina. We present responses from 222 AIDS service and public health organizations. The questionnaire defined rural "as a town, village, or county with fewer than 50,000 people that is not part of a larger metropolitan area." About one-fourth of the clients served by these organizations were female and about one-third were African American. Among the perceived most successful programs provided were outreach efforts, health education and risk reduction, and one-to-one client education or outreach. However, major challenges to providing these programs were lack of funds and a lack of qualified staff. Social stigma, privacy concerns, and community attitudes also presented additional obstacles to providing services in rural areas. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. Utilizing the Clinical Social Work Model to Foster Collaboration among HIV Providers in North Carolina

Author: Lombard, Frank1; Whetten, Kathryn; Forry, Nikki; Despard, Mat1 c/o Whetten -- Health Inequalities Program, Duke U, Durham, NC

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61388053?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article demonstrates one effort to examine the applicability of client behavioral change models to medical provider & case manager behavioral change. The clinical social work model is used to explain the behavioral change process when providers were asked to utilize a voluntary & complex data management system to manage clients' HIV care. Through intense psychosocial support & training initiatives, providers were able to alter their behaviors so that they could successfully integrate new strategies & technologies into their practice while providing better quality care to their clients. 1 Appendix, 22 References. Webcasting: An Innovative Approach to HIV/AIDS Professional Training in Rural Setting

Author: Waddell, Richard D1; Kulig, Raymond P1 Dartmouth Medical School, Lebanon, NH

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61382868?accountid=14925

Abstract: The epidemiology of HIV/AIDS in New Hampshire (NH) mirrors the rest of the nation (New Hampshire Department of Health & Human Services [NHDHHS], 2004); however, the demographics & physical setting of the state are different. With HIV seroprevalence at about 1% & many health care providers working in rural settings with few educational resources available to them, the challenges for keeping providers well informed about HIV/AIDS care & prevention issues are significant. The AIDS Education & Training Center for NH examined several distance learning modalities to determine which would be the best method for the monthly AIDS Seminar Series. The modalities included Real, Media Player & Quicktime, amongst others. This article describes the selection process & our experiences using on-line Webcasts as a way to enhance & achieve successful distance learning for health care providers working in HIV/AIDS in a rural setting. The terms streaming & webcasts are used interchangeably throughout the article. Though streaming is a method of transmission & webcast is an event, they have become intertwined & are often used to describe each other. References. Adapted from the source document. Assessment of HIV-Related School Policy in North Carolina

Author: Fair, Cynthia1; Garner-Edwards, Dara; McLees-Lane, Mary1 Human Services Dept, Elon U, NC cfair@elon.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61375677?accountid=14925

Abstract: This paper presents results from a content analysis of HIV-related school policy in North Carolina. A policy assessment tool was developed and applied based on recommendations drawn from the professional literature. Four major policy content areas were identified: (1) access, (2) confidentiality/communication, (3) medication issues, and (4) HIV education. Of 79 responding school systems, 68 had established policies which were compared to the recommendations acknowledged in the literature. Results indicate a general failure of local school boards to implement the type of detailed HIV-related school policies described in the professional literature. Overall, North Carolina school policies lacked statements referring specifically to HIV/AIDS. Specificity is necessary to protect the rights of HIV-infected children to an education in the least restrictive environment and to protect their confidentiality. It is also necessary for policies to specify details related to HIV education of students and staff. Schools received individualized feedback regarding their HIV/AIDS policy, thereby providing the information necessary for policy improvement. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. Southern Rural African American Faith Communities' Role in STI/HIV Prevention within Two Counties: An Exploration

Author: Brown, Emma J1; Williams, Sharon E1 School Nursing, Coll Health & Public Affairs, U Central Florida, Orlando ejbrown@mail.ucf.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61385612?accountid=14925

Abstract: Purpose: Because of the faith community's historical response to HIV/AIDS, some religious leaders, local community activists, & public health professionals are challenging this institution to get involved in the struggle against HIV especially among adolescents. The paper reviews the historic process & reports findings from a study, which assess Southern Rural Florida pastors' perception of & involvement in STI/HIV prevention education of their adolescent members. Method: Surveys were administered to 43 African-American pastors of three denominations to describe exsisting STI/HIV prevention efforts targeting adolescents within their churches & pastors' perceptions of the STI/HIV & drug prevention needs of their adolescent members. Results: Forty-two percent of the churches had existing educational prevention discussions focused on condom use, pregnancy & STI/HIV prevention, & what constitutes appropriate sexual behavior, while 58% did not. Pastors with programs (83%) & without program (64%) desired specifically designed formal prevention programs. All pastors desired program content consistent with abstinence while 50% & 39% desired additional content on condom use & negotiating safer sex behavior, respectively. Conclusion: The existence of faith-based prevention programs offers hope as churches assume a facilitator role in prevention services;however, their rather narrow & conservative agenda may be perceived as a barrier to effective intervention prevention. Multi-Discipline HIV Longitudinal Training: Utilizing AETC Resources to Build HIV Care Capacity in Minority-Serving Health Centers

Author: Rosen, David1; Abedini, Bonnie R; Jean-Baptiste, Rose; Richetti, Dion; Youngblood, Andrew; Spooner, Linda1 Division AIDS Education, U Medicine & Dentistry New Jersey-Center Continuing & Outreach Education, Newark rosendv@umdnj.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61388074?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article describes a multi-disciplinary HIV clinical training model developed & implemented by the Division of AIDS Education at the University of Medicine & Dentistry of New Jersey (UMDNJ)-Center for Continuing & Outreach Education (CCOE), the New Jersey Local Performance Site (LPS) of the New York/New Jersey AIDS Education & Training Center (AETC). The Longitudinal Training (LT) model combines AETC, Minority AIDS Initiative (MAI) & other available training resources into an adaptable framework that can be implemented by any clinical training entity (e.g., other AETCs, Public Health Training Centers, Center for Mental Health Services grantees, state health departments, STD/HIV Prevention Training Centers, etc.) to provide needs-based, multi-discipline longitudinal training of healthcare providers so as to enhance the HIV care capacity of community health centers serving predominantly minority populations. In addition to describing the AETC & MAI Programs, this paper will discuss the methods used in developing the Longitudinal Training model & will include styles of training, identification of program successes & barriers, a composite case study & continuous outcome measures. Risk Factors for HIV/AIDS among American Indian/Alaska Native Women: One Woman's Story

Author: Fernandez, Angela R1; Keigher, Sharon M; Stevens, Patricia E1 c/o Keigher -- Helen Bader School Social Welfare, U Wisconsin-Milwaukee

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61394270?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article illuminates the main risks that American Indian/Alaska Native women face for contracting the human immunodeficiency virus, & their challenges in living with HIV disease, by presenting a case study of one Native woman's experiences. The woman, a member of a Midwestern Indian tribe, was a participant in a larger in-depth qualitative longitudinal study of 55 women living with HIV. The findings about her path to healing provide insights for understanding & serving this neglected ethnic population, & her words eloquently articulate the struggles of women of color living in dignity with HIV. The article explores potential venues for reaching Native women with culturally relevant prevention & intervention methods, emphasizing the importance of Native culture & spirituality. References. Religious and Spiritual Supports of the Christian African-American HIV-Affected Grandparent Caregiver

Author: Phillips, Irene

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61398662?accountid=14925

Abstract: This qualitative study explores the religious and spiritual supports grandparent custodial caregivers seek from the African-American church. Six female, Christian African-American HIV-affected grandparents living in North Carolina were interviewed. These caregivers perceive God and prayer to be essential to their intrinsic ability to cope. In addition to having the church pray for persons living with HIV/AIDS and their families, the grandparents would like the church to take a more active role in educating the community on HIV/AIDS prevention and in reducing the stigma associated with the disease. They seek little church support for themselves personally, but hope that through the church's efforts, others will not have to endure what they are experiencing. With this knowledge comes better informing HIV/AIDS education program design. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. Therapist's Expectations of Psychotherapy Duration for Individuals Living with HIV

Author: Reece, Michael1; McBride, Kimberly; Shacham, Enbal; Williams, Barbara1 Dept Applied Health Science, Indiana U, Bloomington mireece@indiana.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61391740?accountid=14925

Abstract: Data were collected from 102 psychotherapists providing mental health care to individuals living with HIV at a mental health clinic in the Southeastern United States for the purpose of assessing the perceptions of therapists with regard to necessary therapy duration for a range of mental health issues & disorders. Analyses were conducted to explore the extent to which therapists perceived the need for different therapy durations for individuals living with HIV than those who had not received such a diagnosis. Across all mental health issues assessed, therapists indicated a greater need for therapy duration when an HIV diagnosis was also present, & this generally was consistent without regard to therapist education, demographics, or therapeutic orientation. Given the extent to which mental health care is often a routine component of the HIV social services infrastructure, the findings of this study have important implications for mental health providers, mental health training programs, & the staff & volunteers of other HIV-related organizations. 2 Tables, 34 References. What Is Capacity Building? Lessons from a National Demonstration Program of HIV Education for Social Service Providers

Author: Millery, Mari P1; Messeri, Peter A1 Mailman School Public Health, Columbia U, NY

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61390541?accountid=14925

Abstract: The purpose of this paper is to examine the concept of capacity building & describe an HIV/AIDS education program for social service providers that implemented capacity building in nine ethnic/racial minority communities in the U.S. Quantitative & qualitative evaluation data from the Targeted Provider Education Demonstration (TPED) will be presented to illustrate a capacity building approach. Training-of-trainers (TOT) was a central mechanism for transferring expertise to local communities, & program effectiveness was demonstrated by having 60% of all training programs in the last project year delivered by TOT trainees. Lessons learned about capacity building became a major outcome of the TPED evaluation. It was essential to provide technical assistance & address organizational development issues in participating community-based agencies. These agencies were utilized as a mechanism of rooting the interventions in the local communities in a sustainable way. Reinforcement of inter-organizational linkages facilitated growth of community capacity. Amulti-level model of capacity building that emerged based on the TPED experience is discussed in the context of literature on capacity building. Tables, References. Adapted from the source document. Assessing Clients' Progress through a Permanency Planning Program

Author: Reich, Warren A1; Hudis, Jan1 The Family Center, NY wreich@thefamilycenter.org

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61397116?accountid=14925

Abstract: Eighty-five HIV-positive mothers were tracked in a descriptive analysis of progress through a nonprofit organization's program of legal and mental health services designed to assist ill parents in making a permanency plan for their children after the parent's death. Data on service utilization was analyzed using a Hierarchical Classes (HICLAS) analysis, which uncovered an interpretable pattern of services that clearly distinguished basic (e.g., intake), legal, and clinical services. From the HICLAS output we created an index of progress: [no services] < [basic services only] < [basic + legal services] < [basic + legal + clinical] services. An index of mood was constructed from staff ratings of clients during home visits. Multiple regression analyses revealed that the number of different moods observed in a client was positively correlated with progress. Number of moods was also positively correlated with positive outcome, that is formulating a legal permanency plan and/or transferring care of the child to a new caregiver. Mediation analyses suggested that number of observed moods influences progress, which in turn increases the likelihood of a positive outcome. A second study replicated the prediction of mood on progress in a group of new caregivers who were not mothers. Tables, Figures, References. Adapted from the source document. Do Age and Gender of Social Supports Matter for Low-Income African-American Women Attending an HIV Prevention Program?

Author: Pinto, Rogerio M1; McKay, Mary M1 Center Clinical & Behavioral Studies, New York State Psychiatric Instit, Columbia U

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61433810?accountid=14925

Abstract: Although social support has been linked to improvement in health behaviors, little is known about social support among those most affected by the AIDS epidemic: low-income, young adult, African-American women & their children. Since the age & gender of informal support providers have been shown to influence the types & degrees of social support one receives, this paper examines differences in types & degrees of social support, by age & gender of support persons, received by self-identified African-American female caretakers (n = 197) attending an HIV prevention program. Age of support person was positively associated to overall perceived social & emotional support, as well as to the support person's willingness to help respondents. Adult persons appeared to provide more overall social support to respondents, & to be more eager to help, than did younger persons, even though adult supports made fewer personal contacts. Respondents received significantly more overall social & emotional support from females than from males. Implications for family-based HIV prevention are discussed. 4 Tables, 67 References. Knowledge and Use of AIDS and Aging Services by Older, HIV-Infected Adults

Author: Emlet, Charles A11 U Washington, Tacoma

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61516149?accountid=14925

Abstract: Older adults living with HIV/AIDS require a complex array of services. Such needs can be addressed both by the service network developed for HIV as well as the network developed for older persons. This study of adults, age 50 & over with HIV/AIDS (N = 41), compared the knowledge & use of services commonly available from the HIV network as well as the aging network. The study sample had similar knowledge of HIV services & services designed for older adults. These individuals, however, used a significantly higher number of services provided through the HIV network (mean of 2.61 services) compared to the aging network (mean of .68 services). Predictors for service use varied across systems. While the primary predictor of HIV service use was awareness, Medicaid eligibility, & living arrangements were predictive of use of services from the aging network. 5 Tables, 30 References. An Application of Borrayo's Cultural Health Belief Model to HIV/AIDS Seropositive Hispanics Living along the US/Mexico Border

Author: Keesee, Marguerite1; Ahmad, Ahmad Saleem G; Nelson, Wendy; Barney, David D; Duran, Elizabeth S1 U Oklahoma Center Health Ethics/Reseach/Policy, Norman mkeesee@ou.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61505732?accountid=14925

Abstract: This qualitative study examines the factors that led Hispanic men & women residing along the US/Mexico border to be tested for HIV. These factors were identified by applying Borrayo's (1999) Cultural Health Belief Model (CHBM) for Hispanics using grounded theory methodology. Participants consisted of 32 individuals receiving services from five HIV/AIDS demonstration sites in four states along the US/Mexico border. Results indicate partial support for Borrayo's Cultural Health Belief Model. Results also indicate that Hispanic male participants tended to be directly involved in risky behaviors while the female participants were generally monogamous & unaware of their vulnerability to exposure through the risk behaviors of their male partners. 2 Figures, 42 References. Perceptions of Vulnerability to HIV among Older African American Women: The Role of Intimate Partners

Author: Winningham, April1; Richter, Donna; Corwin, Sara; Gore-Felton, Cheryl1 Center AIDS Intervention Research, Medical Coll Wisconsin, Milwaukee

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61509218?accountid=14925

Abstract: This is one of the first studies among older African American women to examine partner communication & perceived risk for HIV. Understanding that the vast majority of older women are infected through heterosexual contact, this study investigated several partner-related factors associated with perceptions of vulnerability to HIV among older African American women (age range = 50-81 years). A cross-sectional survey was conducted (n = 167) in three rural counties in SC. Perceptions of vulnerability to HIV was most strongly associated with women who were not married, believed that their sexual partner would not approve of using condoms, & had more comfort in communicating with their partners about sex. Recognizing the role of intimate partners in predicting women's perceptions of their own vulnerability to HIV is an important factor to consider when developing HIV prevention programs for older African American women. Future studies need to examine factors associated with effective partner communication, particularly negotiation strategies around condom use among older adults. 3 Tables, 46 References. A Multiple-Family Group HIV Prevention Program for Drug-Involved Mothers and Their Young Children

Author: DeVoe, Ellen R1; Dean, Kara; Joyce, Emily; McKay, Mary M1 School Social Work, Columbia U, New York, NY

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61541648?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article describes the development of a family-based preventive intervention designed to reduce HIV risk behavior in children (8-11 years) whose mothers have a history of drug involvement. The program, HIV-Prevention at the Young Mother's Program with Evolving Families (HYPE), is a 10-week multiple family group intervention influenced by the Collaborative HIV-Prevention & Adolescent Mental Health Project (CHAMP), a family-based HIV prevention program developed to reduce HIV risk behavior & promote mental health of urban pre-adolescents. The development & delivery of HYPE were overseen by a partnership between community members representing an inner-city neighborhood, including staff, administrators, & a graduate of a drug treatment program for mothers in Bronx, New York, & university-based researchers. A description of the collaborative process & an overview of the curriculum from the first pilot program are presented. Implications for family-based HIV prevention for urban populations affected by drug-use & abuse are discussed. 1 Table, 1 Figure, 43 References. Adapted from the source document. Public Sex, HIV Prevention and Law Enforcement

Author: Segovia-Tadehara, Corina D1; Bigler, Mark O; Ferguson, David; Diarte, Jamie1 Dept Social Work, Weber State U, Ogden, UT

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61534975?accountid=14925

Abstract: A sample of 98 men who cruised for sex in public places completed survey questionnaires related to: public sex background, HIV testing, interaction with police, HIV risk behaviors, & demographic information. Fifty-two percent had their first public sex encounter between the ages of 13-23, 76% had been tested for HIV, 15% were HIV positive, 27% had been arrested, & some men engage in sexual behaviors that pose a significant risk for HIV & other sexually transmitted infections, e.g., anal sex w/o condoms. However, outreach efforts & harm reduction methods, where individuals are taught to reduce harmful behaviors, appear to have helped some research participants engage in better decision-making regarding public sex encounters. Continued intervention efforts are needed to assist men who have sex with men (MSM) in making healthful decisions in public sex environments. 3 Tables, 25 References. Midlife Women with HIV: Health, Social, and Economic Factors Shaping Their Futures

Author: Keigher, Sharon M1; Stevens, Patricia E; Plach, Sandra K1 Helen Bader School Social Welfare, U Wisconsin, Milwaukee keigher@uwm.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61547772?accountid=14925

Abstract: Women are the fastest growing population infected with HIV. They are more impoverished, less healthy, & they succumb to AIDS faster than men. Even in WI, a relatively low prevalence state, HIV/AIDS is a significant cause of morbidity & death for women, especially women of color. However, with improved antiretroviral medications & clinical care, many women diagnosed today with HIV will live into old age. What are their prospects for "successful aging?" This essay presents qualitative data on 9 midlife WI women living with HIV to speculate about the probable trajectories & outcomes of their life-courses, highlighting their key security & longevity challenges. Financial resources, social support, health care, & health needs will be critical to their prospects for survival. Only by strengthening basic social provisions for many marginalized groups in the US will women with HIV have the prospect of sharing a future of "successful aging" that many other Americans take for granted. 1 Table, 22 References. Abstract: Grounded theory was used to explore barriers to care & unmet needs for HIV-positive women caring for children under 18 years of age. The main study group consisted of 40 HIV-positive women caring for children. There were 21 Latinas, 11 Euro-Americans, 6 African Americans, & 2 participants from other ethnic groups. "Making It Manageable": Custody Planning with HIV-Affected Families

Author: Mason, Sally1; Vazquez, Deborah1 Instit Juvenile Research, Dept Psychiatry, U Illinois, Chicago smason@psych.uic.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61520599?accountid=14925

Abstract: Custody planning programs for HIV-infected parents help families overcome the obstacles to making future plans for their children, in the event of the parent's debilitating illness or death. In order to understand the social work intervention, progress notes from the Family Options Project, a custody planning program, were analyzed for themes regarding: (1) methods used by the social workers & (2) the content discussed with birth parents. The social workers used problem-solving methods that helped parents examine & partialize problems & explore solutions. Content centered on planning options, parent mental & physical health, & family relationships, reflecting the complexities of planning with HIV-affected families. As people with HIV are living longer, custody planning & family preservation have become equally important outcomes for the program. 2 Tables, 21 References. Adapted from the source document. Reconciling Successful Aging with HIV: A Biopsychosocial Overview

Author: Vance, David E1; Robinson, F Patrick1 Edward R. Roybal Center Research Applied Gerontology, U Alabama, Birmingham devance@uab.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61516194?accountid=14925

Abstract: As HIV is increasingly viewed as a chronic condition, primary care clinicians, case managers, & researchers must address the emerging implications of aging with this disease. Many aspects of HIV overlap with similar aspects of the aging process; however, a synthesis of topics related to both phenomena reveals some unique influences on disease prognosis, which affect the social, psychological, & medical well-being of persons aging with this disease. Fortunately, the gerontological literature is replete with theories, models, & examples of how to age successfully. This literature can inform the topic of aging with HIV in order to provide direction for research, interventions, & services. Trends found within both aging & HIV contexts are used to predict potential difficulties in cognitive decline, depression, social withdrawal, financial planning, immune dysfunction, & mitochondria damage. To understand the biopsychosocial mechanisms surrounding successful aging with HIV, mitochondria damage is used to illustrate the dynamic effects of physical, psychological, & social functioning on each other. Interventions & questions for further research in successful aging with HIV are presented. 1 Table, 1 Figure, 88 References. Adapted from the source document. The Use of Timelines in Illustrating the Permanency Planning Process for Families Infected/Affected by HIV/AIDS

Author: Palmer, Michelle1; Zobel, Melissa1 Consortium Child Welfare's FTP, Washington, DC Mpalmer@Familytiesproject.org

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61554156?accountid=14925

Abstract: The Family Ties Project (FTP) is a multi-agency, collaborative, federally funded demonstration project whose mission is to promote & preserve the well-being of District of Columbia (DC) children, youth, & families affected by HIV/AIDS by working with parents & caregivers to plan for the future care of their children. FTP was first funded in 1996 through the federal Administration for Children, Youth & Families (ACYF) as part of a national grant program to reduce the burden of infants & children abandoned or orphaned as the result of AIDS related illnesses. This article demonstrates the use of timelines in clinical practice. Timelines of the permanency planning process of three families who experienced a maternal death are presented to help illustrate the similarities & differences in the process for each of these families. Additionally, the authors discuss the implications for social work practice as they relate to client/family engagement, length of intervention, interdisciplinary collaboration within the HIV/AIDS service delivery system, & the use of timelines as a clinical tool. 3 Graphs, 13 References. Voices Less Heard: HIV-Positive African American Women, Medication Adherence, Sexual Abuse, and Self-Care

Author: Gant, Larry M1; Welch, Leseliey A1 School Social Work, U Michigan, Ann Arbor Lmgant@umich.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61527516?accountid=14925

Abstract: As the HIV infection rate among African American women steadily increases, the body of research focusing on the lives, health, & concerns of HIV-positive African American women remains insufficient. Through primarily qualitative techniques, this study aims to gain insight into the health issues & concerns of HIV-positive African American women. Focus groups with small numbers of HIV-positive African American women were conducted to understand what factors strengthen & weaken medication adherence for women, determine what women's needs are about these issues, & to find out how medical community & service providers can help meet women's needs about adherence. Results from a series of face-to-face interviews with 98 HIV-positive African American women provided insights on how women with HIV & AIDS take care of themselves, & to what extent various life experiences & events influence self-care & adherence. Focus group findings identified factors that weaken & strengthen current medication adherence. Focus group participants also made several recommendations for future adherence strategies & tactics. Face-to-face interviews demonstrate the impact of substance use & past sexual abuse upon women's efforts to adhere to medication & treatment regimens. Implications & recommendations derived from the analysis provide suggestions for enhancing medication adherence strategies. Enhancing methods of increasing adherence rates for medical & human service professionals can improve support services for African American women living with HIV & AIDS. 4 Tables, 2 Figures, 69 References. Compensating for Cognitive Deficits in Persons Aged 50 and over with HIV/AIDS: A Pilot Study of a Cognitive Intervention

Author: Neundorfer, M M1; Camp, C J; Lee, M M; Skrajner, M J; Malone, M L; Carr, J R1 Myers Research Instit, Menorah Park Center Senior Living, Beachwood, OH mneundorfer@myersri.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61527876?accountid=14925

Abstract: This pilot study describes a cognitive intervention designed to compensate for cognitive deficits among persons with HIV/AIDS aged 50 & over. Seventeen participants were screened for memory & executive function deficits. Ten participants received the intervention, based on Spaced Retrieval (SR) & use of external memory aids. At two months post-treatment, all participants reported that the intervention helped them meet two self-selected functional goals (eg, remembering to take medications, remembering clinic appointments) & the majority successfully learned & retained the correct memory strategy. Spaced Retrieval, coupled with external aids, has potential for helping persons with HIV adhere to treatment regimens. 4 Tables, 47 References. Adapted from the source document. Predictors of Successful Return to Work from HIV-Related Disability

Author: DiClementi, Jeannie D1; Ross, Mary K; Mallo, Connie; Johnson, Steven C1 Dept Psychology, Indiana-Purdue U, Fort Wayne

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61452901?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examines factors distinguishing HIV-infected persons who successfully return to work (RTW) from those who do not. Records were obtained from 135 participants in a return to work program; these were matched with the records of 135 persons who did not return to work. Matching was made on the basis of age, gender, race, CD4 counts, & length of time in treatment. The return to work group had a greater total number of mental health diagnoses than the non-return group; however, when type of diagnosis was controlled for, the non-return group had greater numbers of substance use disorders. It appears that substance use disorders precludes a successful return to the workforce, while accessing the mental health treatment system may actually facilitate a return to work. Careful psychosocial assessment of clients on entry into the primary care clinic can aid referrals directly into a RTW program or into services that would facilitate an eventual return to work. 2 Tables, 12 References. Psychological Distress Patterns of Women and Mothers Presenting for HIV-Related Mental Health Care

Author: Reece, Michael1; Basta, Tania B; Koers, Erin1 Dept Applied Health Science, Indiana U, Bloomington mireece@indiana.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61512761?accountid=14925

Abstract: Over a seven year period, data were collected from 228 women living with HIV upon their self-enrollment into mental health care at an HIV-related mental health clinic. Analyses were conducted to explore the symptoms of mental health distress being presented by women upon their enrollment into care &, in particular, associations between these symptoms & characteristics of motherhood. Findings suggest that women living with HIV present for care with significant levels of psychological distress symptoms & that factors related to parenthood are associated with diverse clinical presentations. As an increasingly prevalent component of the HIV social services system, women living with HIV are in need of access to services that are responsive to their unique mental health issues & that are based upon an understanding of the extent to which familial variables influence psychological distress. 2 Tables, 46 References. HIV and Violence: An Agency-Based Investigation of Behavioral Patterns Associated with Clients Experiencing Current or Past Violence

Author: Herman, Lorette1; Mutchler, Matt G1 AIDS Project Los Angeles lorette@verizon.net

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61492375?accountid=14925

Abstract: Many men & women living with HIV/AIDS are also dealing with current or past violence. HIV/AIDS services agencies would benefit from understanding the relationship & impact of violence & abuse on their specific client population. AIDS Project Los Angeles (APLA), an HIV prevention, advocacy & service agency for those living with & at risk of HIV, decided to investigate client intake questions regarding violence to determine if there were any behavioral patterns associated with those clients who stated they had or were currently living with violence. Substance abuse was an issue in many of those with a history of domestic violence. The implication of this study is that substance abuse, domestic violence & HIV/AIDS programs need to work collaboratively to address these intertwined issues. 1 Table, 3 References. Ryan and Jeanne White: Accidental Advocates

Author: Poindexter, Cynthia Cannon11 Fordham U Graduate School Social Service

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61520554?accountid=14925

Abstract: A discussion of Ryan White & his mother, Jeanne, in light of the upcoming re-authorization of the Comprehensive AIDS Resource Emergency Act. Ryan, a boy with hemophilia who first displayed symptoms of AIDS at the age of 13, became a symbol of the need for that Act. A number of themes in Ryan's life are discussed: facing & fighting the stigma of AIDS; the desire for normalcy; involuntary activism & fame; & being transformed by the experience, especially as he became an articulate advocate & became more aware of the intertwining of AIDS stigma & homophobia. Jeanne White, Ryan's mother, also became of vocal advocate for the rights & needs of people with AIDS. Themes in her life include the transition through phases of caregiving; involuntary activism & fame; being transformed by the experience; & facing & feeling grief. Together, Ryan and Jeanne set an example of how to deal with AIDS in a way that emphasizes action, compassion, & justice. 1 Reference. C. Ong-Dean

Providing Non-Traditional Mental Health Services to Individuals Living with HIV and AIDS: An Evaluation of Five Pilot Programs in Massachusetts

Author: Drainoni, Mari-Lynn1; Johnson, Victoria; McGuire, Jean Flatley; Gerlus, Reginalde1 Center Health Quality/Outcomes/Economic Research, ENRM Veterans Hospital, Bedford, MA drainoni@bu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61556401?accountid=14925

Abstract: The social work model of care is well suited for serving clients with complex mental health & medical issues, including HIV/AIDS. In this article, we present results from an evaluation of five demonstration programs that were established to use professional social workers to provide nontraditional mental health services for individuals living with HIV/AIDS. The results of the evaluation indicate that clients need a range of services that are often not reimbursable through insurance coverage, that it is important to provide services in alternative settings, & that the role of the social worker & the relationship between clinician & consumer are key to the success of the model. 2 Tables, 19 References. Child Care Arrangements of Children Orphaned by HIV/AIDS: The Importance of Grandparents as Kinship Caregivers

Author: Cook, Judith A1; Boxer, Andrew M; Burke, Jane; Cohen, Mardge H; Weber, Kathleen; Shekarloo, Proshat; Lubin, Heidi; Mock, Lynn Owens1 Mental Health Services Research Program, Dept Psychiatry, U Illinois, Chicago cook@ripco.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61543302?accountid=14925

Abstract: This study examined the residential & custody status of children whose mothers were deceased participants in the Chicago Consortium of the Women's Interagency HIV Study (WIHS). Data were collected on 53 minor children. At follow-up, 77% of children lived with family, the residential status of 15% was unknown, & 8% lived in foster care or with a non-relative. Less than 25% of child care arrangements were formalized through legal procedures prior to mothers' deaths. Despite lack of formalized planning or financial assistance, children's relatives (especially grandparents) kept families together, preventing most motherless children from entering the child welfare custodial system. Ways in which health care providers & social workers can engage mothers with HIV/AIDS in the permanency planning process are discussed, as are implications for future research. 1 Table, 42 References. Social-Environmental Influences on HIV Risks: Implications for HIV Prevention

Author: Bhattacharya, Gauri11 School Social Work, U Illinois, Urbana-Champaign gbhattac@uiuc.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61520281?accountid=14925

Abstract: To succeed, HIV prevention programs need to target the multiple factors, at both the individual & social-environmental levels, that have been found to influence HIV risk behaviors & outcomes. An ecological perspective grounded in a population-based public health model is proposed as a conceptual framework for HIV prevention. An overview is presented of the individual-level & social-environmental factors that the empirical literature has documented as influences upon HIV risk behaviors. The proposed framework has a number of implications for social work education, research, training, & policy. This framework calls upon social workers to learn a broad range of cross-disciplinary professional skills, become competent at working with a multidisciplinary team, continually update their knowledge of the ever-changing epidemic, receive organizational support for ongoing training, & obtain critically needed support from policymakers for comprehensive HIV prevention programs. 1 Table, 1 Figure, 42 References. Measuring HIV Services Integration Activities

Author: Messeri, Peter1; Kim, Stephanie; Whetten, Kathryn1 Mailman School Public Health, Columbia U, New York, NY

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61508782?accountid=14925

Abstract: Despite efforts for more than four decades to better integrate the delivery of health & human services, particularly for individuals with problems that cross service sectors, such as HIV, our understanding remains limited as to what successful services integration (SI) strategies look like & whether they translate into improved client health & well being. One obstacle to expanding our knowledge of SI is the absence of a conceptual vocabulary & corresponding measures that delineate the salient features that allow us to compare & contrast the many concrete forms of SI initiatives. Drawing on past research literature, this article presents a conceptual taxonomy for classifying different components of SI initiatives. We present operational measures for the concepts in the taxonomy & illustrate their application for a comparative analysis of services integration activities implemented by 34 HIV service demonstration projects. 7 Tables, 38 References. Stage of HIV-Infection and Social Support Networks: Gay and Bisexual Men's Embeddedness with Family, Friends, and Partners

Author: Vandehey, Michael A1; Shuff, I Michael; Diekhoff, George M1 Midwestern State U, Wichita Falls, TX michael.vandehey@mwsu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61517098?accountid=14925

Abstract: Previous research on social support networks of HIV-infected persons has reported differences in amount of social contact (embeddedness) across stages of illness. In the present study, 75 HIV-infected gay & bisexual males from a statewide, Midwestern sample listed up to nine social support providers & rated their frequency of contact & perceived level of social supportiveness with each person. Comparisons were made between the frequency of contact with social supports at each stage of illness (asymptomatic, symptomatic, & AIDS) & at initial, 1-year, & 2-year time intervals. Frequency of contact was similar across the stages of illness & over time. Furthermore, 'very supportive' ratings increased from 76% to 81% over the two-year time period despite an increase in AIDS diagnoses. It is concluded that progression of the illness does not guarantee reduced contact with or lowered positive perceptions of social relations. 5 Tables, 43 References. Adapted from the source document. Treatment Adherence among Clients in AIDS Service Organizations

Author: Demmer, Craig11 Dept Health Services, Lehman Coll, CUNY, Bronx cdemmer@lehman.cuny.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61548090?accountid=14925

Abstract: For social workers in AIDS service organizations (ASOs), helping clients adhere to antiretroviral therapies is a priority, since non-adherence can significantly reduce drug efficacy & lead to drug resistance. This preliminary study examined treatment adherence among HIV-infected individuals receiving services from four ASOs in New York City. Adherence was suboptimal; more than one half of the sample had not taken their medications as prescribed within the past month & 20% had missed taking one or more pills the previous day. Clients who reported greater adherence considered themselves to be in good emotional health, had not used illegal drugs recently, & believed that their ASO was very helpful with regard to treatment adherence. Regular assessment of clients' treatment adherence is essential & social workers should continue to be involved with other health professionals in designing, implementing, & evaluating interventions for promoting adherence among clients in ASOs. 4 Tables, 26 References. Adapted from the source document. COPIES ARE AVAILABLE FROM: HAWORTH DOCUMENT DELIVERY CENTER, The Haworth Press, Inc., 10 Alice Street, Binghamton, NY 13904-1580

Risky Sexual Behaviors and HIV/Disease Knowledge of Injection Drug Users Attending Needle Exchange Programs: A Call for Additional Interventions

Author: Ksobiech, Kate11 Urban Studies Program, U Wisconsin, Milwaukee kksobiec@mcw.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61505625?accountid=14925

Abstract: A meta-analysis of worldwide Needle Exchange Program research was conducted with 14 studies, looking at the impact of needle exchange programs (NEPs) on the sexual risk behaviors & HIV/disease knowledge of injection drug users. Based on the limited data available to date (a total of 14 studies), two conclusions were drawn. First, HIV/disease knowledge does increase with NEP attendance. Second, risky sexual behaviors do not appear to decrease with NEP attendance, & may in fact increase. Neither of these areas has been investigated nearly as often as risky drug behaviors among NEP attenders. Focusing on disease knowledge & reducing risky sexual behaviors of NEP clients are both key areas for AIDS Service Organization staff to consider when developing HIV risk reduction interventions for injection drug users (IDUs). 3 Tables, 54 References. When Spider Webs Unite: The Work of Volunteers in Providing Home-Based Care in Namibia

Author: Steinitz, Lucy Y11 Catholic AIDS Action, Windhoek, Namibia lucy@caa.org.na

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61507372?accountid=14925

Abstract: Resource-limited countries with high rates of infection face the biggest challenges in addressing the HIV/AIDS pandemic, as government & nongovernmental infrastructures are often undeveloped & slow to shift their focus away from nation-building & other perceived priorities to scale up their response to HIV/AIDS. This article examines the role of trained volunteers who provide Home-Based Family Care in the high-HIV-prevalence, resource-limited country of Namibia in Southern Africa. While each volunteer alone can make only a small difference, an organization that effectively uses many volunteers can make a large impact. Special focus is given to the experience -- & potential -- of churches to recruit, train, & help sustain volunteers to provide AIDS care & support. The case example of Catholic AIDS Action in Namibia -- that country's largest nongovernmental organization dedicated to HIV-related prevention & care -- is used to illustrate key points. The article concludes with "lessons learned" that can be applied to other nongovernmental & faith-based providers who seek to recruit & involve volunteers in the provision of direct-care services. 1 Table, 1 Appendix, 18 References. African American Women, AIDS, and Resiliency

Author: Weinberg, Beth A1; Simoni, Jane M1 Ferkauf Graduate School Psychology, Yeshiva U baweinberg@aol.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61556402?accountid=14925

Abstract: Ongoing shifts in the populations affected by the AIDS epidemic are reflected in the increasing proportion of AIDS cases in women -- particularly women of color. While African American women represent approximately 13% of the US female population, in 2000, 63% of all reported AIDS cases in women in the United States were among African American/non-Hispanic women. Despite these statistics, relatively few studies in the area of HIV research target African American women exclusively. As part of a larger study, we asked 105 African American women to describe in their own words how their lives have changed since becoming HIV positive. Despite multiple past & current stressors, the majority of participants provided responses that were decidedly positive. Clinical implications as well as future research directions based on these findings are discussed. 23 References. Adapted from the source document. A Telephone Support Group Intervention for Non-HIV-Infected Fathers of HIV-Infected Children

Author: Battles, Haven B1; Wiener, Lori S; Lewis, Eleesha M; Patel, Rita; Grant, Shelma Middleton1 HIV/AIDS Malignancy Branch, National Cancer Instit, National Instits Health, Bethesda, MD battlesh@mail.nih.gov

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61501452?accountid=14925

Abstract: Fathers of HIV-infected children are rarely targeted as a group for specific psychosocial support & intervention. This paper is intended to provide an in-depth case description of a specific telephone group intervention & its perceived effectiveness by group members. Themes covered during the group intervention included diagnosis disclosure, coping, relationship issues, stigma, need for social & concrete support, medical care, & losses. The group was perceived to be effective in bringing together fathers of HIV-infected children. The fathers came from geographically diverse regions & without the group they might not have the opportunity to talk openly with others. All of the participants believed the group to be a valuable resource & would recommend it to others. The findings suggest that this is a cost-effective intervention that provides support to people who are either geographically distanced from each other or who are not emotionally ready to participate in a face-to-face support group. 31 References. Staying Present with Suffering -- Images Still and Moving

Author: Bertman, Sandra L11 Graduate School Social Work, Boston Coll, MA sbertman@comcast.net

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61496851?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article expands on a technique used in educational & clinical settings to stimulate the creative imaginations of practitioners & persons living with HIV/AIDS. By jolting us out of our ruts of ordinary perception, the use of visuals with clients & colleagues leads to new awareness, the generation of creative options & problem-solving possibilities, increasing empathy & healing. A repertoire of case studies from the visual & literary arts & from popular culture is being compiled to elicit concerns, introduce concepts, enhance/build skills, explore attitudes, & to promote healing. The technique is offered as a practice tool & readers are invited to submit responses, examples, & images from their own experience. 5 Figures, 10 References. Adapted from the source document. Abstract: The article reports on findings from an exploratory survey of 25 HIV/AIDS workers.
