Responding to Employment Needs: Strategies for Supporting Individuals with HIV/AIDS

Author: Timmons, Jamie Ciulla1; Fesko, Sheila Lynch1 Instit Community Inclusion, U Massachusetts, Boston ajtimmons@earthlink.net

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61449046?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article reports on a study investigating employment-related support to individuals with HIV/AIDS from the perspective of service providers. Surveys were completed by AIDS Service Organizations (ASOs) & Vocational Rehabilitation (VR) administrators & counselors that examined the extent to which support around employment was provided, the type of work-related services delivered, & outreach practices offered to this population. Findings indicated that although employment is becoming an increasingly important concern to this population, service providers have a limited capacity to respond to such needs. Strategies are presented for social workers & other professionals to consider as they work to support individuals with HIV/AIDS with employment-related concerns. 1 Table, 22 References. Author: Wolf, Ed11 AHP (UCSF AIDS Health Project, San Francisco)

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61448871?accountid=14925

Abstract: Describes the HIV Timeline, a tool used to educate those providing health & social services to AIDS clients. Developed by the AIDS Health Project (AHP) at the U of California San Francisco, the Timeline documents major events in the progress of AIDS & the search for a treatment since 1980, including reported cases, research breakthroughs, legal & political issues, & worldwide death rates (en masse & of particularly notable persons). Rural HIV/AIDS Services: Participant and Provider Perceptions

Author: Roeder, Kevin R11 Social Work Professional Program, U Wisconsin, Green Bay

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61548884?accountid=14925

Abstract: An exploratory study to investigate the needs of those living with HIV to determine related service challenges was conducted in 1997 & replicated in 1999. This study utilized qualitative & quantitative measures, surveyed both individuals who are living with HIV & HIV/AIDS service providers in predominately rural counties of a Midwestern state with a low incidence of HIV infection. Respondents' perceptions were solicited regarding existing services in the areas of: (1) awareness; (2) satisfaction; & (3) gaps & barriers. Findings indicate: (A) the critical role AIDS service organizations have in rural service delivery; (B) service awareness is much stronger among those living with HIV disease than HIV/AIDS service providers; (C) satisfaction with available services is growing; & (D) there is a widening gap between important needs & available services. 4 Tables, 24 References. Uncertainty and Quality of Life: Psychosocial Realities of Combination Anti-Retroviral Therapy

Author: Shernoff, Michael11 Hunter Coll Graduate School Social Work mshernoff@aol.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61502653?accountid=14925

Abstract: The advent of combination anti-retroviral therapies has ushered in a new phase of the AIDS epidemic with resulting changes in the psychological & emotional complexities for people living with HIV & AIDS. The medical uncertainties regarding whether, when, & which drugs to begin or stop is paralleled by a constellation of psychosocial issues that has left both clients & mental health providers scrambling to adjust to the new world of HIV disease as a chronic & manageable illness for most people living in developed countries. Paramount among the emotional & psychological issues that need to be addressed are quality of life issues & how these influence a person's medical decision making & other crucial life choices. This article contains personal as well as professional reflections from the perspective of an HIV-positive psychotherapist who has worked in the field of HIV/AIDS since the onset of the epidemic. The advent of combination anti-retroviral therapies has ushered in a new phase of the AIDS epidemic with resulting changes in the psychological & emotional complexities for people living with HIV & AIDS. The medical uncertainties regarding whether, when, & which drugs to begin or stop is paralleled by a constellation of psychosocial issues that has left both clients & mental health providers scrambling to adjust to the new world of HIV disease as a chronic & manageable illness for most people living in developed countries. Paramount among the emotional & psychological issues that need to be addressed are quality of life issues & how these influence a person's medical decision making & other crucial life choices. This article contains personal as well as professional reflections from the perspective of an HIV-positive psychotherapist who has worked in the field of HIV/AIDS since the onset of the epidemic. 1 Table, 15 References. Mandalas as a Therapeutic Technique for HIV-Infected Children and Adolescents: What Do They Reveal?

Author: Wiener, Lori S1; Battles, Haven B1 National Cancer Instit, National Instits Health, Bethesda, MD wienerl@mail.nih.gov

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61546855?accountid=14925

Abstract: HIV positive children need a safe outlet to express their feelings about living with the virus. Creative therapeutic techniques such as the creation of mandalas (circle drawings) can facilitate the development of an emotional outlet. Carl G. Jung used mandalas in his work & believed that they are the paths to our center, the voice of our unconscious. The content of 32 mandalas created by children & adolescents living with HIV during clinical sessions with their social worker was analyzed for the themes expressed & colors utilized. The most prominent themes within the artwork concerned the concrete aspects of living with HIV. However, negative themes were balanced by themes concerning happiness, optimism, social relationships, & the normalcy of everyday life. The artwork illustrates that HIV plays a prominent role in the lives of the participants. Their resiliency is evidenced by the fact that nearly half of the themes expressed were positive & life affirming. 3 Tables, 1 Figure, 19 References. Adapted from the source document. Ethnicity and Multiple Sex Partners: An Application of the Health Belief Model

Author: Neff, James Alan1; Crawford, Sharon L; MacMaster, Samuel A1 School Social Work, U Texas, Austin jneff@mail.utexas.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61449052?accountid=14925

Abstract: The Health Belief Model posits that risk behavior is influenced by perceptions of susceptibility & severity of risk, as well as perceived benefits & barriers to risk reduction. This study replicated & extended earlier analyses (Neff & Crawford, 1998), which found racial/ethnic differences in relationships between Health Belief Model variables & HIV risk behaviors. In the 1998 analysis, negative paths between susceptibility & risk behaviors among African American males were interpreted as "denial" of HIV risk. In the replication a self-report of number of sex partners served as a measure of HIV risk rather than the composite risk measure used previously. Analyses using this alternative risk measure do not indicate "denial" of risk. Susceptibility was positively related to number of partners in all racial groups. Indirect effects of susceptibility upon number of partners via alcohol disinhibition effects were found only among Mexican Americans, suggesting interventions should emphasize individual risk perceptions & alcohol expectancies. Understanding these relationships between race/ethnicity & HIV risk behaviors may help design culturally specific intervention & service programs. 2 Tables, 1 Figure, 35 References. Adapted from the source document. COPIES ARE AVAILABLE FROM: HAWORTH DOCUMENT DELIVERY CENTER, The Haworth Press, Inc., 10 Alice Street, Binghamton, NY 13904-1580

Emerging Roles for Persons Affected by HIV in Health Care Program Planning and Administration: Consumer Advisory Boards

Author: Rollet, Christine1; Winiarski, Mark G1 Dept Psychiatry & Behavioral Sciences, HIV Mental Health Research Group, Montefiore Medical Center, Bronx, NY crisrollet@aol.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61496331?accountid=14925

Abstract: Involving HIV-affected consumers in meaningful advisory roles in a health care program, research planning, & administration now receives increased attention from funders & program developers. Establishing an advisory board is one common method for obtaining consumer input. While program managers may view an advisory board as cumbersome, benefits can be derived by programs & consumers. The experience of creating an advisory board, staffed by social workers, for a federally funded HIV mental health program in the south Bronx, provides lessons for social workers, program developers, & administrators regarding issues such as recruiting an unbiased panel, accommodating non-English speakers, management support of the board, & the coordination of several boards mandated by funders. 25 References. The Role of Community in Meeting the Needs of African-American HIV-Affected Families

Author: Mason, Sally11 Dept Psychiatry, U Illinois, Chicago smason@psych.uic.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61498339?accountid=14925

Abstract: Using qualitative methods, university researchers enlisted the participation & collaboration of community residents to assess the service needs of HIV-affected families in an inner-city African American community with a high seroprevalence of HIV/AIDS. Community members infected with & affected by HIV/AIDS participated in interviews & reviewed & refined the preliminary findings. The participants were concerned by the lack of HIV/AIDS community services that were family-sensitive, ie, addressed the stresses & concerns of those people who provide support & care for a person with HIV. Participants identified community awareness & education as critical to serving those infected & affected. Denial & stigma in the community need to be addressed concurrently with the development of specific services for HIV-affected families. 20 References. Adapted from the source document. Tuesdays with Fellow Travelers: A Psychoeducational HIV/AIDS-Related Bereavement Group

Author: Pomeroy, Elizabeth C1; Holleran, Lori K1 School Social Work, U Texas, Austin

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61496357?accountid=14925

Abstract: This project examined the effectiveness of a psychoeducational grief group intervention for AIDS-related death & bereavement. Family members & friends who had lost a loved one to HIV/AIDS participated in a six-week, time-limited bereavement group. Depression, anxiety, & grief reactions were analyzed using standardized instruments. Qualitative methods were also employed. Findings suggest that a psychoeducational bereavement group may be an effective intervention for caregivers who have unresolved emotional issues related to the loss of a loved one due to HIV/AIDS. 2 Tables, 35 References. More than Shelter: Benefits and Concerns for People in HIV/AIDS Housing

Author: Smith, Laurie A1; Pynoos, Jon1 School Social Work, U Nevada, Las Vegas

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61540794?accountid=14925

Abstract: Housing people with HIV/AIDS is a pressing need as poor & disenfranchised groups are increasingly infected. Advocates, planners, & direct care workers lack information on the impact of HIV/AIDS housing from the perspective of residents. This study interviews 57 persons in subsidized housing solely for people with HIV/AIDS. Findings indicate that stable housing made significant contributions to improvements to the health, medication adherence, mental health, & social support of the residents. Problems noted in the housing include behavior associated with drug & alcohol abuse. Differences in social support gains by subgroups, particularly women, are also noted along with recommendations for increased housing availability using this & other models. 3 Tables, 14 References. Serodiscordant Couples in the Age of Combination Therapies: Challenges and Coping Patterns

Author: Beckerman, Nancy L11 Wurzweiler School Social Work, Yeshiva U, New York, NY desilu@ix.netcom.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61429668?accountid=14925

Abstract: Serodiscordant couples have faced a number of challenges over the decades of HIV/AIDS. Combination therapies added hope to the survival of the HIV positive partner, but it also has created new challenges to intimacy. What are the unique challenges, dyadic conflicts, & ways of coping for these couples of mixed HIV status? This article reports on the findings from an exploratory study of twelve serodiscordant couples (both heterosexual & gay) from three different states, & five different AIDS organizations. The three emotional challenges to their relationship were identified as (1) the impact of side effects from medications, (2) safer sex conflicts, & (3) the decision-making process regarding reproductive issues & family planning. When asked how they coped, the primary source of psychological support was found in their friendships with other couples of mixed HIV status, couple counseling, & individual counseling. 23 References. Author: Newman, Peter A.

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/62302993?accountid=14925

Abstract: Asserts that the social context of coming out may be vital to the overall psychological health and well-being of gay youth and to their HIV risk behavior. Coming out is a multifaceted developmental process. Interventions targeting anti-gay attitudes and behaviors in families, schools, and communities, in addition to combating discriminatory social policies, may be vital in HIV prevention for gay youth. A Practice Tool for Dialogue about HIV and Social Services

Author: Bertman, Sandra L11 U Massachusetts Medical School sbertman@attbi.com

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61543262?accountid=14925

Abstract: This article presents a technique used by the author to stimulate the therapeutic imaginations of practitioners & persons living with HIV/AIDS in educational & clinical settings. Narrative responses to four visual case studies raise themes of intergenerational issues, coping with life-limiting illness, & caregiver burden allowing for enhancement of assessment & empathic skills. This method often leads to the generation of creative options & problem-solving possibilities. The technique is offered as a practice tool & readers are invited to submit responses, examples, & images from their own experience. 4 Figures, 5 References. Adapted from the source document. Coming Out in a Hostile Social Environment: Challenges for HIV Prevention among Young Gay Men

Author: Newman, Peter A11 UCLA Neuropsychiatric Instit, Center Community Health, Los Angeles, CA panewman@ucla.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61498127?accountid=14925

Abstract: Young people under age 24 account for almost 50% of all new HIV infections in the US; young men who have sex with men are disproportionately represented among this population (Centers for Disease Control, 2000). The major assertions of this article are that (1) the social context of coming out may be vital to the overall psychological health & well-being of gay youth &, in turn, to their HIV risk behavior; & (2) coming out is a multifaceted developmental process -- different pathways of coming out may be related to different types & determinants of HIV risk behavior. Evidence of a milieu of stigmatization, antigay hatred, harassment, & violence among gay youth suggests that merely addressing HIV risk with even high-quality individual-level interventions that myopically focus on sexual behavior may be insufficient. Interventions targeting antigay attitudes & behaviors in families, schools, & communities, as well as combating discriminatory social policies & institutions, may be a vital aspect of HIV prevention among gay youth. 54 References. HIV Prevention for Active Injection Drug Users: A Brief History of Syringe Exchange Programs

Author: MacMaster, Samuel A1; Womack, Bethany G1 Coll Social Work, U Tennessee, Nashville

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61538720?accountid=14925

Abstract: It is estimated that approximately 50% of all new HIV infections are related to injection drug use (Holmberg, 1996), as used syringes can harbor blood with traces of viable HIV for over 4 weeks (Abdala, Stephens, Griffith, & Heimer, 1999). Due to this concern, Syringe Exchange Programs (SEPs) & other related HIV prevention programs for active injection drug users have been developed & expanded in many urban areas of the US over the past 15 years. Based on the Harm Reduction model of service provision, SEPs seek to reduce the harm associated with injection drug use through the elimination of the primary means by which HIV risk is spread, the shared syringe. Despite extensive research supporting the efficacy of SEPs, significant barriers exist to the delivery of these services, as SEPs are often viewed as being outside the traditional continuum of care for HIV/AIDS &/or substance abuse. All social workers involved in HIV/AIDS services should be aware of these programs, & continued expansion & integration with other HIV/AIDS services should be a priority. 75 References. "Twists and Turns": Case Study of an HIV Caregiving Journey

Author: Poindexter, Cynthia Cannon11 School Social Work, Boston U, MA ccp@bu.edu

http://search.proquest.com.proxy.lib.wayne.edu/docview/61498157?accountid=14925

Abstract: Twists & turns are commonly felt by caregivers over the volatile trajectory of HIV disease; they report extraordinary challenges alongside existential transformations. This case study presents the complex experience of one HIV-affected mother who negotiated the psychological spaces of dying & caregiving while occupying the same physical space with a critically ill son during an extremely stressful & sad time. Comments from the research respondent are included as endnotes. This story raises questions about the nature of the care relationship & suggests the utility of story telling for HIV caregivers. 37 References. Adapted from the source document.
